Title: Spin-Charge Groups for Fermions in Fluids and Crystals: General Structures and Physical Consequences

URL Source: https://arxiv.org/pdf/2603.18125v1

Published Time: Fri, 20 Mar 2026 00:10:36 GMT

Number of Pages: 86

Markdown Content:
## Spin-Charge Groups for Fermions in Fluids and Crystals: General Structures and Physical Consequences 

Arist Zhenyuan Yang 1, 2, 3 and Zheng-Xin Liu 1, 2, ∗

> 1

School of Physics and Beijing Key Laboratory of Opto-electronic Functional Materials and Micro-nano Devices, Renmin University of China, Beijing, 100872, China 

> 2

Key Laboratory of Quantum State Construction and Manipulation (Ministry of Education), Renmin University of China, Beijing, 100872, China 

> 3

Department of Physics and HK Institute of Quantum Science & Technology, The University of Hong Kong, Pokfulam Road, Hong Kong, China 

(Dated: March 20, 2026) Known symmetry groups are not sufficient to handle various couplings between spin, charge and spatial degrees of freedom for fermions. To fill in this gap, we introduce the spin-charge groups (SCG) to describe the most general symmetries of fermionic systems. These groups are composed of the spin and charge operations as the ‘internal’ symmetries, the spatial and temporal operations as ‘external’ symmetries, couplings between them, and projective twists. After providing the general group structures of SCG, we study their potential applications in concrete physical systems, including 3He superfluids, charge-4e superconductors, collinear magnets with spin-fluxes, and superconductors with coexisting magnetic orders. We show that SCG can give rise to extra band degeneracies, Chern numbers and cross spin-charge responses like spin-supercurrents. Hence SCG provide a route to classify and explore new phases of matter even when strong interactions are included. 

Introduction.— Symmetry plays essential roles in de-scribing physical laws as well as classifying phases of matter. For instance, the various crystals in nature are categorized into 230 classes due to their discretized space group symmetries[1]. These spatial symmetries, together with time reversal, protect numerous kinds of topological band insulators[2–12], semimetals[13–17] and superconductors[18–21]. The representations of these groups, on the other hand, provide an important tool to describe quasiparticles that may have no counterparts in high energy physics[22–25]. In magnetic systems, 1651 magnetic space groups[26] were introduced to describe different types of magnetic orders. Since magnetic mo-ments are mainly contributed by spins, when spin-orbit coupling is negligible, a huge number of new magnetic matter, including the altermagnets [27–29], emerge with symmetries described by spin space groups[30–35]. Even though, the SSGs do not exhaust the symmetries of mag-netic systems. For instance, spin flux[36–39] in collinear magnets can give rise to projective twist in the symmetry operations which are not included in SSGs. For itinerant electrons in crystalline materials, such as insulators and metals, the symmetries are described by double space groups or double magnetic/spin space groups[24, 26]. But the symmetry groups of supercon-ductors (SCs) are more subtle because the particle-hole degrees of freedom give rise to a SU(2) charge operation group[40, 41]. When considering the coupling between charge and lattice operations in SCs and including mag-netic flux[42] (dubbed ‘charge flux’ later), the resulting symmetry groups are called projective symmetry groups (PSGs)[43, 44] which were first introduced to describe the parton Hamiltonian of quantum spin liquids. However, for systems having coexisting SC order and magnetic or-der – as observed in the Iron based SCs[45–47] or heavy fermion SCs[48–50] – the possible couplings between spin, charge and lattice degrees of freedom fall out of the scope of known symmetry groups. This reveals a new realm of symmetries, whose physical consequences may go beyond our present knowledge of electronic systems. The com-plete description of these symmetries is challenging due to the complex intertwinings between spin, charge, and spatial/temporal operations. In the present work, we introduce spin-charge groups (SCG) to incorporate these new classes of symmetries. A SCG contains internal and combined symmetry opera-tions simultaneously acting on lattice, spin and charge degrees of freedom. Furthermore, central spin and charge operations can projectively twist the space-time operations, resulting in different projective classes char-acterized by invariants of the second group cohomol-ogy. SCG can unify the description of many important electronic systems, including charge-4e superconductors, spin-twisted magnets, and superconductors with coexist-ing magnetic order, etc. We then demonstrate that SCG can be used to explore new phases of matter and new physical properties, such as manipulating pairing sym-metries and spin supercurrents in superconductor. 

SO (4) and internal symmetries.— Electron opera-tors admit a representation in terms of four Majorana fermions. Thus, the maximal connected group acting on the spin and charge degrees of freedom of electrons is 

SO (4) = ( SU (2) s × SU (2) c)/Z2[25, 41], where the spin rotation group SU (2) s and the charge rotation group 

SU (2) c are generated by Pauli matrices 12 σx,y,z and

For a given electron system, two types of internal sym-metries can be distinguished: spin-only and charge-only. The corresponding groups, denoted as S0 and C0 respec-tively, consist of symmetry operations acting solely on the spin and charge degrees of freedom. For instance, in Bardeen-Cooper-Schrieffer type spin-singlet SCs[51, 52], one has S0 = SU (2) s and C0 = Zf 

> 2

. In the super-fluid A-phase of 3He[53, 54], S0 = Zf 

> 2

> 2

, their ‘product’ is given by ( S0 × C 0)/Z2,which will be denoted by S0 ⋆ C0 ≡ (S0 × C 0)/Z2 later. In general, the group S0 ⋆ C0 does not coincide with the full set of internal symmetry operations that act trivially on the lattice and leave the system invariant, since spin and charge operations may be intertwined. We denote the full internal symmetry group as Of , which is a normal sub-group of the complete symmetry group Gf . Accordingly, one has Of ⊆ SO (4) and ( S0 ⋆C0)◁Of , with the quotient group Of /(S0 ⋆ C0) characterizing the coupling between spin and charge operations. Structure of Spin-Charge Groups.— We now formu-late the general structure of a SCG Gf . We first con-sider Gf as unitary. Since Of ◁ Gf , depending on the quotient group Gb = Gf /O f , Gf can be a spin-charge space group (SCSG), a spin-charge point group (SCPG), or a spin-charge Euclidian group (SCEG). Gf is an ex-tension of Gb by Of . Meanwhile, due to the nontriv-ial center Z(Of ) ⊇ Zf 

> 2

, a SCG contains two essential information[57–60]: (I) the global action of Gb on Of ,and (II) the projective twist of Gb by 2-cocycles in Z(Of ). We first focus on sector (I) and neglect the twist. For fermionic systems, the global action on Of is realized by associating l ∈ Gb with element ϕl ∈ N (Of ), where 

N (Of ) is the normalizer of Of within SO (4), N (Of ) ⊆

SO (4). We write Gf in forms of cosets of Of , namely

Projective twists in the spin sector.— If Sz is con-served, namely if U (1) s ∈ Of , the extension of Gb can contain projective twists in the spin sector that are miss-ing in SSGs[32–34]. The inclusion of spin twist is equiv-alent to introduce background U (1) s spin-gauge field, namely spin fluxes[39]. Two types of spin fluxes can be distinguished, namely the ferromagnetic (FM) fluxes and anti-ferromagnetic (AFM) fluxes. The FM spin flux is similar to the usual charge-flux due to the spin polariza-tion, while the AFM spin flux is more exotic. Here we illustrate the physical consequence of AFM spin flux via a square-lattice model with Gb = p4mm 1′

and Of = U (1) s ⋆ U (1) c. For simplicity, we assume that the information of the charge sector is trivial. With basis 

C† = ( c†↑, c †↓), we consider the tight-binding Hamiltonian

Interestingly, upon including next-nearest-neighbor hopping terms (explict form given in the SM), the gT and 

gMx,y symmetries are explicitly broken but their combi-nation gT M x,y are unbroken. As a result, the generic 4-fold degeneracies are lifted, except along the high-symmetry lines kx = 0 and ky = 0 (see Fig. 1). The remaining fourfold degeneracies are owing to the survived invariants ( gTy gT M x )2 = −T2y and ( gTx gT M y )2 = −T2x.Moreover, due to the broken gT and the presence of AFM spin-flux, the resulting band structure can carry a nonzero Chern number C = 2 at half filling, thereby realizing quantum anomalous Hall effect.

Because the effective mirror gMx reverses the Berry curvature, cases I and II correspond to topologically trivial SCs. By contrast, case III realizes a topologi-cal SC with Chern number ν = ±4. Upon introduc-ing next-nearest-neighbor hopping, the symmetry gMx is broken in cases I and II, and nonzero Chern numbers 

ν = ±2, ±4, ±8 emerge. Incorporating spin-fluxes there-fore provides an effective route to realizing topological SCs. Meanwhile, due to the presence of gC4 , all these bands exhibit fourfold degeneracy at the Γ point due to the following 4-dimensional irreps

Neumann-Constrained Cross Response. The SCPG symmetry dictates the physical linear responses of the system. The coupling between spin, charge and orbital degrees of freedom in SCG can give rise to cross responses

Here we consider another cross response, i.e. spin-supercurrent induced by vector po-tential AAA, namely Jzi = ρzij Aj in Sz -conserved supercon-ductors on square lattice. Due to Neumann’s principle, the tensor ρzij should be invariant under the action of SCPG, whereas the index i is covariant under lattice and charge rotation, the index z subject to spin operation, and the index j varies under both lattice and charge operations. Conclusion.— We introduced the concept of SCG – spin-charge groups to describe the most general symme-try groups of fermions in fluids or crystals. After figuring out the group structure via extension of space-time sym-metry groups by spin and charge operations allowing pro-jective twists, we discussed several typical systems whose symmetries are described by SCGs, including superfluids, charge 4 e-superconductors, anti-ferromagnets containing 6

spin-fluxes, and superconductors with coexisting AFM order. We illustrate that the spin-flux can give rise to in-teresting degeneracies and topological bands structures. Especially, superconducting AFM can host cross spin-charge responses like spin-supercurrents induced by mag-netic potential. The SCG opens a symmetry-based route toward exploring phases of matter with exotic physical properties.

S1. SOLVING SPIN-CHARGE COUPLING FROM GOURSAT’S LEMMA 

We introduce the so-called Goursat’s lemma, and use it to uncover the spin-charge coupling structure in the internal symmetry group Of .

Lemma 1 (Goursat’s lemma) Let H ⊂ G1 × G2 be a subgroup such that pr i(H) = Gi for i = 1 , 2, where pr i denotes the natural projection onto Gi. Then H uniquely determines two normal subgroups N1 ⊴ G1, N2 ⊴ G2, and a group isomorphism 

φ : G1/N 1 → G2/N 2.

Here, N1 is defined as H ∩ G1, where G1 is viewed as a subgroup of G1 × G2, namely the kernel of pr 2. Similarly, we have N2 = H ∩ G2. Moreover, there are natural isomorphisms: 

G1/N 1 ≃ H/ (N1 × N2) ≃ G2/N 2.

For a proof, see page 8 of Serre’s book [1]. Now, we can obtain the following

(ii) SOf /U (1) s = {U (1) s, e −iσ xπ/ 2U (1) s}, COf /Z4c = {Z4c, e −iτ xπ/ 2Z4c}.

Correspondingly, there are two possible forms of Of . The first is Of = [ E + e−i(σx+ 12 τz )π/ 2]U (1) s ⋆ Z4c, while the second is Of = [ E + e−i(σx+τx)π/ 2]U (1) s ⋆ Z4c.More examples of Of for given S0 and C0 are provided in Tab. I of the main text. 3

S2. RIGOROUS RESULTS FOR GROUP EXTENSIONS VIA GROUP HOMOMORPHISMS 

˜ϕ : Gb → N (Of )/O f

Recall that, given two groups Of and Gb, an extension of Gb by Of is a group Gf such that Of is a normal subgroup of Gf and Gb is the corresponding quotient. The solutions to the extension problem, with Of and Gb fixed, describe all consistent ways of coupling Of and Gb, and can be further classified in terms of the so-called coupling homomorphisms between them. A coupling homomorphism is a group homomorphism ˆϕ from Gb to Out (Of ) = Aut (Of )/ Inn (Of ). For a given extension Gf , the associated coupling homomorphism is induced by the conjugation action of Gf on Of ,modulo inner automorphisms of Of . More precisely, let 

E : 1 → Ofi

−→ Gfπ

−→ Gb → 1be a group extension, and let s be a section of π, that is, a set-theoretic map satisfying π ◦ s = id Gb . Then one obtains a set-theoretic map ˆϕs : Gb → Aut( Of ) defined by ˆϕs(gb)( of ) = s(gb) of s(gb)−1, gb ∈ Gb, o f ∈ Of . (S2) The mapping ˆϕs satisfies the property ˆϕs(gb1 ) ˆϕs(gb2 ) ˆϕs(gb1 gb2 )−1 ∈ Inn( Of ), or, equivalently, the section s satisfies 

s(gb1 ) s(gb2 ) s(gb1 gb2 )−1 ∈ Of . (S3) Thus, upon passing to the quotient via p : Aut( Of ) → Out( Of ), this construction induces a homomorphism ˆϕ ≡ p ◦ ˆϕs : Gb

> ˆϕs

−−−→ Aut( Of ) p

−−→ Out( Of ),

namely, the coupling homomorphism. It is straightforward to verify that the coupling homomorphism ˆϕ is independent of the choice of the section s. An extension is called central if Of is contained in the center of Gf , which is only possible when Of is abelian. For any central extension, one has Inn (Of ) = {E}, and hence Out (Of ) = Aut (Of ). In this case, the corresponding coupling homomorphism ˆϕ : Gb → Aut( Of ) is trivial. In our context, we naturally assume that for each gb ∈ Gb, there exists an element ϕgb ∈ SO (4) such that ˆϕs(gb)( of ) = s(gb) of s(gb)−1 ≡ ϕgb of ϕ−1 

> gb

.

This assumption reflects the requirement that the conjugation acts within the spin and charge degrees of freedom, whose maximal connected symmetry group is SO (4). It follows that ϕgb ∈ NSO (4) (Of ), the normalizer of Of in SO (4). Hence, the set-theoretic map ˆϕs induces a map 

ϕ : Gb → NSO (4) (Of ), with ϕ(gb) = ϕgb .

Furthermore, the condition in Eq. (S3) implies that ϕ satisfies 

ϕgb1 ϕgb2 ϕ−1 

> gb1gb2

∈ Of . (S4) Therefore, upon passing to the quotient, one obtains a group homomorphism ˜ϕ : Gb → NSO (4) (Of )/O f ,

which is independent of the choice of ˆϕs. In the following, we restrict our attention to group extensions whose coupling homomorphisms ˆϕ arise from homomorphisms ˜ϕ : Gb → NSO (4) (Of )/O f .Detailed information of Of with familiar S0 and C0, including their center, NSO (4) (Of )/O f and corresponding physical systems are collected in Tab.S1.

> 2

> 2

H2(C4, Z f 

> 2

Accordingly, the symmetry groups for the s-wave and d-wave superconductors correspond to the trivial extension, namely the direct product, whereas the symmetry group for the ( px ± ip y )-wave superconductors corresponds to the nontrivial extension. We now consider the homomorphism ˜ϕ : C4 → SU (2) /Zf 

> 2

= SO (3) . (S9) Given such a homomorphism ˜ϕ, one can construct a map ϕ : C4 → SU (2) by lifting ˜ϕ to SU (2). Consequently, the induced coupling homomorphism ˆϕ is trivial. This implies that nonconjugate group homomorphisms ˜ϕ : C4 → SO (3), for instance those with different kernels, can nevertheless give rise to the same trivial coupling homomorphism ˆϕ. In other words, the trivial coupling homomorphism can be realized in multiple inequivalent ways. However, this distinction becomes irrelevant when one considers only the resulting group extensions, since all such constructions lead to central extensions. As a consequence, one obtains central extensions that are isomorphic as groups but arise from inequivalent coupling homomorphisms. This situation occurs for the s-wave, ( px ± ip y )-wave, and d-wave superconductors. Let B0 denote the kernel of ˜ϕ. From Eq. (S9), B0 can take only one of three values: C4, C2, or E. These correspond to rotations in SO (3) with angles θ = 0, π, or ∓π/ 2, respectively, associated with the generator C4. Upon lifting from 

SO (3) to SU (2), the corresponding angles in SU (2) become 0, π/ 2, and ∓π/ 4, respectively, consistent with Eq. (S7).         

> B. Obstruction Free of Extensions Induced by ˜ϕ:Gb→N(Of)/O f

It is well known that not every homomorphism from Gb to Out (Of ) can serve as a coupling homomorphism for a group extension, due to the presence of cohomological obstructions[ 1, 2]. Here, we show that all group homomorphisms 

Gb → Out( Of ) that arise from homomorphisms Gb → NSO (4) (Of )/O f are obstruction-free. 

Proposition 1 The coupling homomorphism ˆϕ : Gb → Out (Of ) induced by ˜ϕ : Gb → NSO (4) (Of )/O f is obstruction-free. 

Proof Choose a lift ϕ : Gb → NSO (4) (Of ) of ˜ϕ, and define 

f (g1, g 2) := ϕ(g1)ϕ(g2)ϕ(g1g2)−1 ∈ Of , ∀ g1, g 2 ∈ Gb.

Since each inner automorphism of NSO (4) (Of ) induces an automorphism of Of , the lift ϕ defines a mapping ˆϕs : Gb →

Aut( Of ), and one has 

ˆϕs(g1) ˆϕs(g2) = Inn[ f (g1, g 2)] ˆϕs(g1g2), (S10) 6

where Inn denotes the inner automorphism 

1 → Z (Of ) → Of

> Inn

−→ Aut( Of ) → Out( Of ) → 1,

and Z(Of ) is the center of Of .The obstruction cocycle ω3 is detected by the associativity of ˆϕs(g1) ˆϕs(g2) ˆϕs(g3) for g1, g 2, g 3 ∈ Gb, namely

h ˆϕs(g1) · f (g2, g 3)

with ω3(g1, g 2, g 3) ∈ Z (Of ).Hence, we have

C. Solutions to group extensions with arbitrary kernels 

Group extensions can be classified rigorously within the mathematical literature[ 2, 3]. Here, we translate the abstract mathematical formulation of these solutions into the framework adopted in the main text. In the following, we continue to use the notation introduced in the previous section. 

1. When Of is abelian. 

In this case, ˜ϕ induces a homomorphism ˆϕ : Gb → Aut (Of ). The isomorphism classes of extensions of Gb by Of are in one-to-one correspondence with the elements of the second cohomology group H2ˆϕ(Gb, O f ). In particular, under this correspondence, the split extension, where Gf is the semidirect product of Gb and Of , corresponds to the trivial element 0 ∈ H2ˆϕ(Gb, O f ). Different abstract realizations of Gf can be obtained by specifying the multiplication law on the set Of × Gb, as follows: (o1, g 1) ( o2, g 2) =

2. When Of is non-abelian. 

In this case, ˜ϕ induces a homomorphism ˆϕ : Gb → Out (Of ) without any obstruction, and the isomorphism classes of extensions are classified by H2ˆϕ(Gb, Z(Of )), where Z(Of ) denotes the center of Of . However, unlike the case where Of is abelian, there is no natural correspondence between isomorphism classes and elements of H2ˆϕ(Gb, Z(Of )). Instead, the distinctions between isomorphism classes are determined by elements of H2ˆϕ(Gb, Z(Of )). Thus, to obtain all solutions for Gf , one initial solution is required, and all other solutions can be derived sequentially by inserting elements from H2ˆϕ(Gb, Z(Of )) into this initial solution. D. Solutions realized by introducing site-dependent SO (4) operations

A. The Second Group Cohomology 

The extension include the projective twist whose factor systems are classified by the second group cohomological

(Gb, A ), where A is the center of Of , namely A = Z(Of ), and φ is the action on the coefficient group A induced by the conjugation of ϕ(Gb) and will be discussed in section S4 A 3. The group Of is the kernel of a spin-charge group SCG as an extensions of the ‘physical’ group Gb.

2. Decoupling of cohomological twist 

When Z(Of ) is a product group A = As × Ac and if the action of Gb on A does not include exchanging the generators, then the group cohomology can be decoupled, 

H2

> φ

(Gb, U (1) × Z4) = H2 

> φs

(Gb, U (1)) × H2 

> φc

(Gb, Z 4),H2

> φ

> φs

(Gb, U (1)) × H2 

> φc

(Gb, U (1)) ,H2(Gb, Z 2 × Z2) = H2(Gb, Z 2) × H2(Gb, Z 2).

Here the φs and φc stands for action of Gb on the spin and charge coefficient groups respectively, where the nontrivial actions mean taking inversion for the coefficient (for group U (1) and Z4). In the second equation we used the conclusion that H2

> φ

> φ

(Gb, U (1)). For the coefficient group

> 3. Action of φ(Gb)on the coefficient group A

The coefficient group A is a Gb module with action φ, where φ is determined by the map ˜ϕ : Gb → N (Of )/O f or simply by the representative ϕ(Gb) ⊂ N (Of ). For unitary element l ∈ Gb, the action of l on A is defined by ˜ϕl or 

ϕl ∈ N (Of ). Supposing ω2(l1, l 2) ∈ A, then one has 

φl · ω2(l1, l 2) = ϕlω2(l1, l 2)ϕ−1 

> l

. (S22) Notice that there exist a more-to-one correspondence from ˜ϕ to φ.For anti-unitary groups, we adopt the definition of time reversal operation with T = e−i(σy +τy ) π 

> 2

K such that T

commutes with all of the spin-charge operations in SO (4). In this convention, ϕT is defined on top of e−i(σy +τy ) π 

> 2

such that the action of T on A is fully determined by ϕT ∈ N (Of ), 

φT · ω2(l1, l 2) = ϕT e−i(σy +τy ) π 

> 2

Kω 2(l1, l 2)Ke i(σy +τy ) π 

> 2

ϕ−1

> T

= ϕT ω2(l1, l 2)ϕ−1 

> T

,

which agrees with the action of unitary elements in (S22). Similarly, for any anti-unitary element l ∈ Gb, ϕl is defined on top of e−i(σy +τy ) π 

> 2

such that (S22) applies for anti-unitary groups. 

Sometimes it is more convenient to adopt another convention for the section of anti-unitary elements l ∈ Gb with 

ϕ′ 

> l

= ϕ(l)e−i(σy +τy ) π 

> 2

. The advantage of this convention is that ϕ′ 

> l

is the complete spin-charge operation associated with 

l. For instance, when ϕ′ 

> T

= E, the ‘pure time-reversal’ operation reads T ′ = K. Notice that the time-reversal operation 

T ′ in this new convention is not commuting with SO (4) anymore. 

If an unitary group Gb and an anti-unitary group G′ 

> b

have the same multiplication table, they are said to be pseudo-isomorphic. If they have the same action on a coefficient group A, then one has 

H2

> φ

(G′

> b

, A ) = H2

> φ

(Gb, A ).

Namely, the classification only depends on the action φ, no matter if the group is unitary or anti-unitary. However, as discussed in section S4 C, the representation theory for anti-unitary groups are very different from unitary ones even if their invariants (i.e. projective classes) are the same.   

> B. Invariants of H2
> φ(Gb, A )

In the following, we will provide the invariants (namely the generators of the second group cohomology H2

> φ

(Gb, A )with Gb abstract groups, A abelian coefficient groups A = U (1) , Z 4, Z 2, Z 2 × Z2 and φ the various actions of Gb on A.From these invariants, one can obtain the invariants of the original cohomology H2

> φ

 Gb, Z(Of )). When φ is trivial action on the coefficient groups A = U (1) , Z 4, Z 2, then the invariants for the projective classes are given in Tab.S7. When the nontrivial action of φ is taking inversion (or complex conjugation) for coefficient groups A = U (1) , Z 4,then the invariants for the projective classes are given in Tab.S8. When the nontrivial action of φ is exchanging the generators of the coefficient group A = Z2 × Z2, then the invariants for the projective classes are given in Tab.S9.   

> C. Projective Representations of Gb: a few Examples

In this section, we provide concrete examples to illustrate the invariants and the generalized projective representations in the corresponding class. We focus on the cases in which Gb is a magnetic point group. According to the unitarity/anti-unitarity and the action φ on the spin and charge coefficients, we label the elements of Gb in the following way: (i) an anti-unitary element g is labeled by g′ as the usual notation of magnetic groups; (ii) an element g which flips the spin σz is labelled by adding a superscript ˜ g;(iii) an element g which reverses the charge τz is labelled by adding a superscript ˙ g.For instance, for the magnetic point group 2′2′2 = {E, C 2xT, C 2y T, C 2z },65

1. Example-1: Gb = C2 = 2 = {E, C 2} ∼= Z2

Trivial action φ = E

Tab.S7 list the invariants for trivial action φ = E. The trivial action include two different cases: (I) unitary group Gb = 2 with ϕ(Gb) = E. There is no need to distinguish the spin coefficient group and the charge coefficient group. The classification reads 

H2(2 , U (1)) = Z1, H2(2 , Z 2) = H2(2 , Z 4) = Z2, (S23) where nontrivial twist exists for the coefficient groups Z2 and Z4.For A = U (1) , Z 2, Z 4, the trivial class corresponds to 1-dimensional linear reps of Gb = 2, namely, D(C2) = ±1. For the Z2 coefficient group, the nontrivial class is characterized by the invariant ˆC22 = ω2(C2, C 2) = −1, and the corresponding nontrivial projective rep reads 

D(C2) = ±i, 

which can be realized by the representative ϕ(C2) = e−iσ z π 

> 2

> 2

ω2(C2, C 2) = ±i), and the corresponding nontrivial projective rep is given by

> 2

> S5. DETAILED DERIVATIONS OF RESULTS IN THE MAIN TEXT A. Spin-charge groups for 3D fermionic superfluids
> 1. Gb=O(3) ×ZT
> 2and Of=Zf
> 2

In this case, spin-charge groups are characterized by the following continuous homomorphisms ˜ϕ : O(3) × ZT 

> 2

→ SO (3) s × SO (3) c. (S27) We now show that such continuous homomorphisms fall into exactly 36 inequivalent class. 

Lemma 3 Any continuous homomorphism ρ : SO(3) → SO(3) is either trivial or an automorphism. 

Proof Consider the differential at the identity, 

dρ e : so (3) −→ so (3) .

Since so (3) is simple, dρ e is either 0 or injective. If dρ e = 0 , then ρ has discrete image; since SO (3) is connected, the image must be trivial. If dρ e̸ = 0 , then it is an isomorphism. Hence ρ is a local diffeomorphism onto an open subgroup of the connected group SO (3) , so it is surjective. Its kernel is a discrete normal subgroup of SO (3) , hence central; but the center of SO(3) is trivial. Therefore the kernel is trivial and ρ is an automorphism. 

Lemma 4 Every continuous automorphism of SO(3) is inner. 

Proof This is standard: Aut (so (3)) ∼= SO (3) , and the center of SO (3) is trivial. Hence every Lie-group automorphism of SO(3) is conjugation by some element of SO(3) .

Proposition 2 Up to conjugation equivalence, there are exactly three inequivalent continuous homomorphisms 

˜ϕ : SO(3) × ZT 

> 2

−→ SO(3) .

Denote elements in SO (3) × ZT 

> 2

by (Rn(θ), T ϵ) with ϵ ∈ { 0, 1}. Then the three homomorphisms are represented by 

˜ϕ0(Rn(θ), T ϵ) = E , ˜ϕ1(Rn(θ), T ϵ) = [ Rz (π)] ϵ, ˜ϕ2(Rn(θ), T ϵ) = Rn(θ).

Proof Let ˜ϕ be such a homomorphism and set 

ρ(Rn(θ)) = ˜ϕ(Rn(θ), 1) , t = f (E , T ).

Because T commutes with SO (3) , t commutes with ρ(SO(3)) ; moreover t2 = E .If ρ is trivial, then ˜ϕ is determined by the involution t. Up to conjugacy, there are only two possibilities for an involution: E and a π rotation, which we denote by Rz (π). This gives ˜ϕ0 and ˜ϕ1.If ρ is nontrivial, then by the previous lemmas it is an inner automorphism. Up to conjugacy, we may assume 

ρ(Rn(θ)) = Rn(θ). Since t commutes with all of SO (3) and the center of SO (3) is trivial, we must have t = E . Hence 

˜ϕ2(Rn(θ), T ϵ) = Rn(θ).

These three classes are inequivalent because they have different restrictions to the identity component and, when the restriction is trivial, different images of T .

We now consider O(3) ×ZT 

> 2

= SO (3) ×{ E, P }× ZT 

> 2

. Every g ∈ O(3) has a unique decomposition g = ( −1) η(g)( det g)g

with η(g) = 1−det g 

> 2

∈ { 0, 1}. Fix two commuting π rotations Rz (π), R x(π) ∈ SO(3) about perpendicular axes. Then, 

Proposition 3 Up to conjugation equivalence, there are exactly six inequivalent continuous homomorphisms 

˜ϕ : O(3) × ZT 

> 2

−→ SO(3) ,

represented by 

˜ϕ1(g, T ϵ) = E , ˜ϕ2(g, T ϵ) = Rz (π)ϵ, ˜ϕ3(g, T ϵ) = Rz (π)η(g),

˜ϕ4(g, T ϵ) = Rz (π)η(g)+ ϵ, ˜ϕ5(g, T ϵ) = Rz (π)η(g)Rx(π)ϵ, ˜ϕ6(g, T ϵ) = (det g)g. 

All exponents are understood modulo 2.75 

Proof Let ˜ϕ be such a homomorphism. Restrict it to the connected subgroup SO (3) , and denote the restriction by ρ.If ρ is nontrivial, then by the previous lemmas it is an inner automorphism. Then up to conjugacy, we may assume 

ρ(Rn(θ)) = Rn(θ).

Now both P and T commute with the SO (3) . Hence ˜ϕ(P, 1) and ˜ϕ(E , T ) centralize all of ρ( SO (3)) = SO (3) . Since the center of SO(3) is trivial, both must be E. Therefore 

˜ϕ(g, T ϵ) = (det g)g, 

which gives ˜ϕ6. If ρ is trivial, then ˜ϕ is determined by a commuting pair of involutions 

p = ˜ϕ(P, 1) , t = ˜ϕ(E , T ).

If p = E , then up to conjugacy t is either E or Rz (π). This gives ˜ϕ0 and ˜ϕ1. If p̸ = E , conjugation equivalence lets us normalize p to Rz (π). Then t must lie in the centralizer CSO(3) (Rz (π)) . Hence, there are exactly three possibilities: 

t = E , t = Rz (π), t = Rx(π).

These give g3, g4, and g5.

Theorem 1 Let Φ = { ˜ϕ1, ˜ϕ2, ˜ϕ3, ˜ϕ4, ˜ϕ5, ˜ϕ6} defined in Prop. 3. Up to the conjugation equivalence, the continuous homomorphisms

Proposition 4 Every continuous homomorphism ˜ϕ : SO (2) → O(2) is conjugate equivalent to a unique representative of the form 

˜ϕn(R(θ)) = R(nθ ), n ∈ Z≥0.

Hence the inequivalent classes are indexed by the nonnegative integer n.

Proof Because SO (2) is connected, its image under any continuous homomorphism is connected, hence lies in the identity component. Therefore 

˜ϕ(R(θ)) = R(f (θ)) 

for a continuous homomorphism f : R/2πZ → R/2πZ. Such homomorphisms are exactly 

f (θ) = nθ, n ∈ Z.

So every homomorphism is of the form R(θ) 7 → R(nθ ). Conjugation by the reflection sends n 7 → − n. Thus n and −n

are equivalent. Distinct values of |n| are inequivalent because they have different kernels (equivalently, different degrees on the circle). Therefore the classes are labeled by n ∈ Z≥0.76 For each continuous homomorphism ˜ϕ : SO (2) × ZP 

> 2

→ O(2), the restriction of ˜ϕ to the connected component 

SO (2) ⊂ O(2) has the form ˜ϕ(R(θ)) = R(nθ )) for a unique integer n ≥ 0, up to conjugacy. Moreover, the relation ˜ϕ(P R (θ)P −1) = ˜ϕ(R(θ)) = R(nθ ) = ˜ϕ(P ) ˜ϕ(R(θ)) ˜ϕ(P )−1 = ˜ϕ(P )R(nθ ) ˜ϕ(P )−1. (S28) implies that for n > 0, ˜ϕ(P ) ∈ SO (2) and for n = 0, ˜ϕ(P ) ∈ O(2).

Since the group contains the continuous subgroup U (1), the most convenient way to prove this fact is the so-called Hamiltonian approach[ 4, 5 ]. The Hamiltonian approach is a numerical method for decomposing linear or projective representations into irreducible ones: one constructs a random complex Hermitian matrix that is invariant under the conjugation actions of the representation matrices, and each distinct eigenvalue of this matrix labels an irreducible representation, with its degeneracy equal to the dimension of the corresponding irreducible representation. For our case, we first select the discrete subgroup Z8 ⊂ U (1), generated by z0 = I4 ⊗ e−iπσ z /4. Then, we construct the desired matrix according to Algorithm 1. As a result, the distribution of the eigenvalues of the random Hermitian matrix H is 4 + 4. The corresponding irreducible representation is the following: When adding next-nearest-neighbor hopping terms, the gT symmetry is broken (so gC2 gT is) and the gT gMx,y 

symmetry is preserved. From the symmetry point of view, the band rep for U (1), gTx and gTy at an arbitrary momentum point contains four two-dimensional irreducible representations, which can also be proven by the Hamiltonian approach. 78 However, along the high-symmetry lines kx = 0, there is an extra symmetry
